First, finish your 90-day habit, even if you do not finish “all of English.” If you started the daily 20-minute routine from Chapter 10, protect it. Two minutes of sound warm-up. Six minutes of targeted decoding. Eight minutes of adult-appropriate connected reading. Four minutes of reread and log. That routine was designed to produce repeated accurate encounters without making your days collapse into schoolwork.

Second, widen your reading diet carefully. Do not jump from decodables straight into a dense website and then use the struggle as “proof” you are stuck.

FREE TO KEEP - TO EMAIL TO OTHERS. – COURTESY OF GLOBAL SOVEREIGN UNIVERSITY

Global Sovereign University Press Page: 133

That is the thin ice problem from Chapter 9.3, just in adult clothing. Instead, build a bridge the same way you were taught to build bridges.

Choose one real text you want: a work email, a clinic message, a form, a news paragraph, or a short article. Underline three words that stop you. Turn those into your practice words for the week, using the methods you already have.

If the words are short, stay in the map, blend, check, and adjust. If the words are long, use preview, chunk, decode, connect, and start peeling affixes when you see them.

Then rewrite one or two sentences into a bridge version that is more decodable, practice that for a few days, and return to the real sentence. This is not avoidance. This is training under control.

Third, use tools as mirrors, not as rescues. If you are practicing alone, keep the order from Chapter 10.3: attempt, check, map back, and reread. GENO can confirm after you try. Speech-to-text can reveal what you actually said. A voice recording can show you where you skipped, substituted, or rushed. But the technology should make you more methodical, not more dependent. If you notice yourself tapping to hear words before you try, you are not building mapping. You are building a new version of “just tell me.”

Your next steps, if you are teaching a child

If you are a parent, tutor, or teacher, the invitation is a little different. You are not only learning the code. You are building the environment that trains a child’s identity as a reader.

Protect three things.

First, protect sequence and spiral. The scope matters, but the spiral matters more than most people think. Chapter 11.3 warned you: children do not learn in tidy increments. Every new pattern needs contrast, mixed practice, and decodable reading where that pattern actually appears.

Second, protect decodability in real reading. If your child is doing great in word lists and then “reading” a book by predicting from pictures and sentence patterns, you are training for the wrong job. Leveled predictable text can be a guessing factory. The anti-guessing rule matters: “That would make sense, but let’s check the letters.” Calm, boring, technical. You are not punishing. You are teaching what reading is.

Third, protect dignity and joy as competence, not as entertainment. Chapter 9.2 told the truth: some phonics programs crush motivation. The fix is not to abandon explicit instruction. The fix is to make sure the child experiences the payoff early and often. That means short decodable passages that say something, rereading that creates smoothness, and meaning questions that keep reading connected to communication.

If a child hits a plateau, return to the Chapter 9 diagnostics you already learned to ask: Is it stable a week later in mixed text? Are they stuck in practice land? What do they do when stuck? Those questions keep you in the engineering mindset and out of blame.

How to continue, starting today

Read something tonight that is short enough to finish and controlled enough to win. A paragraph. A note. A few sentences you wrote yourself. Use your routine when you get stuck. Check after you try. Map back to the letters. Reread once.

Then write one line in your log, like Chapter 10.1 taught: what you practiced, what tripped you, and what you will target next.

That is not busywork. That is the Helix: sound, code, application, glue.

Global Sovereign University Press Page: 137

1. The Phonics Reference Card

One page. Read it. Keep it where you read.

For one-syllable words: Map · Blend · Check · Adjust

•​ MAP. Look at the letters. Group them into graphemes — the written units that represent single sounds. One letter (t), two letters (sh), three letters (tch), or four (igh). •​ BLEND. Say each grapheme's sound smoothly enough that they connect into a word. Not c-a-t. /k/ /a/ /t/. •​ CHECK. Does what you said match a real word you know? If yes, move on. •​ ADJUST. If not, try the other lawful sound for the trickiest grapheme. Usually it's the vowel. Try again. For multisyllabic words: Preview · Chunk · Decode · Connect

•​ PREVIEW. Look across the whole word. Find the vowels. Each vowel sound starts a syllable. •​ CHUNK. Split between consonants. Peel familiar prefixes and suffixes. The remaining piece is usually a root you can decode. •​ DECODE. Apply the syllable types to each chunk. Closed syllable = short vowel. Open syllable = long vowel. Silent-e = long vowel. Vowel team = team sound. R-controlled = the r dominates. Consonant-le = schwa + l. •​ CONNECT. Say each chunk smoothly and pull the whole word together. If it sounds wrong, smooth one syllable's vowel and try again. Check meaning. Move on. Print this page. Keep it where you read. This is the whole code in eight moves.

FREE TO KEEP - TO EMAIL TO OTHERS. – COURTESY OF GLOBAL SOVEREIGN UNIVERSITY Page 138: Dr. Gene A Constant - Author

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 2. The 44 Phonemes of English

And the most common spellings of each

English has approximately 44 distinct sounds — 24 consonant phonemes and roughly 20 vowel phonemes (including diphthongs and r-controlled vowels). Each one can be spelled multiple ways. This chart shows the most useful spellings to learn first.

Phoneme Common spellings Examples

/b/ b, bb bat, rabbit

/d/ d, dd, ed dog, ladder, mailed

/f/ f, ff, ph, gh fun, stuff, phone, laugh

/g/ g, gg, gh go, egg, ghost

/h/ h, wh hat, who

/j/ j, g, ge, dge jet, gem, large, badge

/k/ k, c, ck, ch, q kite, cat, back, school, queen

/l/ l, ll lap, ball

/m/ m, mm, mb man, hammer, lamb

/n/ n, nn, kn, gn no, dinner, knee, gnat

/p/ p, pp pen, apple

/r/ r, rr, wr rat, carrot, write

/s/ s, ss, c, ce, sc sun, miss, city, dance, scene

/t/ t, tt, ed top, butter, walked

/v/ v, ve van, give

/w/ w, wh win, what

/y/ y, i (in -ion) yes, onion

/z/ z, zz, s, se zoo, buzz, dogs, please

/ch/ ch, tch chip, watch

/sh/ sh, ti, ci, ssi ship, action, special, mission

/th/ (voiceless) th thin, math

/th/ (voiced) th this, mother

/o/ short o, a hot, want

/ā/ long a_e, ai, ay, eigh cake, rain, day, eight

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 3. The Six Syllable Types

How to know which vowel sound to try

When you chunk a long word, each chunk is a syllable. Each syllable has a type. The type tells you which vowel sound to try first.

Type Pattern Vowel sound Examples

Closed Vowel + consonant ending the syllable

Short cat, hop-ping, sub-mit

Open Vowel at the end of the syllable

Long he, go, ba-sic, ti-ger

Silent-e (VCe)

Vowel + consonant + silent e Long cake, hope, com-plete

Vowel team Two vowels working together Varies — learn the team

rain, boat, fee, head

R-controlled Vowel followed by r The r dominates

car, her, fir, for, hurt

Consonant-le Consonant + le at end of word

Schwa + l ta-ble, pur-ple, can-dle

Most multisyllabic words mix two or three types. Decide each chunk on its own. If your first try sounds wrong, switch one syllable's vowel and try again. That is adjusting.

FREE TO KEEP - TO EMAIL TO OTHERS. – COURTESY OF GLOBAL SOVEREIGN UNIVERSITY

Global Sovereign University Press Page: 141

4. Vowel Patterns Quick Reference

The most reliable patterns to expect

If you can recognize these twenty patterns on sight, you can decode the vowel in 90% of English words you encounter. Practice them in the order they appear.

Pattern What it does Examples

Single short vowel (CVC)

a/e/i/o/u says its short sound cat, pen, sit, hop, bus

Silent-e (VCe) Long vowel + consonant + silent e cake, theme, kite, bone, cute

Open syllable Vowel ends the syllable; says long sound

be, go, hi, my, mu-sic

Vowel team ai Says long ā, usually middle of word

rain, brain, paid, train

Vowel team ay Says long ā, usually end of word day, play, stay, gray

Vowel team ee Says long ē see, tree, sleep, three

Vowel team ea Says long ē most often; sometimes short e

eat, sea / head, bread

Vowel team oa Says long ō boat, road, soap, coat

Vowel team ow Says long ō at end of word; /ow/ in middle

snow, low / cow, down

Vowel team oo Long /oo/ as in moon; short /oo/ as in book

moon, soon / book, look

Diphthong oi Middle of word coin, boil, point

Diphthong oy End of word boy, toy, joy

Diphthong ou Middle of word out, loud, found

Diphthong ow End of word cow, how, brown

r-controlled ar The vowel + r dominates car, far, star, smart

r-controlled or The vowel + r dominates for, born, short, sport

r-controlled er/ir/ur All three say the same /er/ sound her, bird, fur

Trigraph igh Three letters, one long-ī sound high, night, light

Trigraph eigh Three letters, one long-ā sound eight, weight, sleigh

-y at end Says long ē after consonant happy, sunny, baby, lady

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 5. The 90-Day Adult Phonics Plan

Summary card for adult learners working independently

The full plan lives in Chapter 10. This card is the bones. Print this so you can stay on the path even when you don't remember exactly what you read.

Daily structure — 20 minutes, six days a week

•​ 2 minutes — Sound warm-up. Practice three to five target phonemes from Section 2. •​ 6 minutes — Targeted decoding. Pick a pattern from Section 4. Decode 10 to 15 words containing that pattern. Use Map · Blend · Check · Adjust. •​ 8 minutes — Connected reading. Read a short passage that contains the pattern you just practiced. Use bridge sentences from your real life when you can. •​ 4 minutes — Reread and log. Read the passage a second time for smoothness. Write one line: what tripped you, what you will target next.

Phase 1 (Days 1–30): The reliable core

•​ Closed syllables and short vowels. CVC words. •​ Single consonants and consonant blends. •​ Common digraphs: sh, ch, th, wh, ph, ck.

Phase 2 (Days 31–60): The long vowels and teams

•​ Silent-e pattern (VCe). •​ Common vowel teams: ai, ay, ee, ea, oa, ow, oo. •​ Diphthongs: oi/oy, ou/ow.

Phase 3 (Days 61–90): R-controlled and multisyllabic

•​ R-controlled vowels: ar, or, er/ir/ur. •​ Two-syllable words. Chunking with the six syllable types. •​ Begin previewing for prefixes and suffixes. Move toward Volume 3. If you miss a day, do not start over. Pick up where you left off. The plan is a path, not a test.

Global Sovereign University Press Page: 143

6. The Children's Phonics Routine

Daily structure for teaching a child, from Chapter 11

Designed so a tired parent can run it after dinner without preparation. Ten to fifteen minutes per session. Four or five sessions a week beats two long ones.

Open with sound work — 3 minutes

•​ Three to five phonemes the child has been learning. Hear it, see it, say it. •​ Two segmenting tasks: "What sounds do you hear in fish?" /f/ /i/ /sh/. •​ Two blending tasks: "/r/ /e/ /d/. What word?" red.

Teach the pattern — 4 minutes

•​ Show today's grapheme or pattern. Name it. Show the sound. •​ Decode 5 to 8 words containing the pattern. Slow, then smooth. •​ Spell 2 to 3 of those words on paper or with magnetic letters.

Apply in connected reading — 5 minutes

•​ Read a short decodable passage that uses today's pattern. Not a leveled book — a decodable one. •​ If the child stalls, prompt the routine, not the answer: "What sound? Try." •​ Reread once for smoothness.

Close with meaning — 2 minutes

•​ Ask one comprehension question that requires the meaning, not the words. •​ End on a real comment about effort. "You decoded chipped without help. That's new this week." Consistency beats intensity. Fifteen minutes a day for ten weeks will change a child's reading more than a one-hour session once a week for the same period.

FREE TO KEEP - TO EMAIL TO OTHERS. – COURTESY OF GLOBAL SOVEREIGN UNIVERSITY Page 144: Dr. Gene A Constant - Author

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 7. Phonics Glossary

Plain-language definitions of every technical term used in this book

Listed alphabetically. Refer to this when a term appears in a chapter and you want a quick reminder of what it means. Alphabetic principle. The idea that written English represents the sounds of spoken language. Letters and letter patterns stand for phonemes. This is the foundation phonics is built on. Blending. Saying the individual sounds in a word smoothly enough that they connect into a recognizable word. /k/ /a/ /t/ blended becomes cat. Closed syllable. A syllable that ends in a consonant. The vowel inside says its short sound. Cat, hop, sub-mit. Consonant. Any letter representing a sound made by partly or fully blocking air in the mouth. There are 21 consonant letters and 24 consonant phonemes in English. Consonant blend. Two or three consonants together where each keeps its own sound. Black, splash, and stream. CVC word. Consonant-Vowel-Consonant. Cat, sit, and hop. The simplest word shape that teaches the closed-syllable pattern. Decodable text. A passage written so that every word can be sounded out using the patterns the reader has already been taught. The opposite of leveled or predictable text. Diphthong. A vowel sound that glides from one position to another within a single syllable. /oi/ in coin. /ow/ in cow. Digraph. Two letters representing one sound. Sh, ch, th, ph, wh, ck, and ng. Explicit instruction. Teaching where the rule is shown and named directly, not left for the learner to discover. The opposite of "figure it out from context." Grapheme. A written unit — one letter or a group of letters — that represents a single phoneme. T is a grapheme. Sh is a grapheme. "Tch" is a grapheme. Long vowel. A vowel sound that is the same as the letter's name. The "a" in "cake." The o in go. Open syllable. A syllable that ends in a vowel. The vowel says its long sound. He, go, ba-sic. Orthographic mapping. The brain's process of bonding a word's spelling to its pronunciation so that future encounters become instant. Decoded words become sight words through this process. Phoneme. The smallest unit of sound in a spoken word. /k/ /a/ /t/ are three phonemes. Phonemic awareness. The ability to hear and manipulate individual phonemes in spoken words. Lives entirely in the air, no letters required.

FREE TO KEEP - TO EMAIL TO OTHERS. – COURTESY OF GLOBAL SOVEREIGN UNIVERSITY

Global Sovereign University Press Page: 145

Phonics. The instructional system that teaches the connection between letters and sounds — the way the alphabetic principle works in practice. R-controlled vowel. A vowel followed by r, where the r changes the vowel sound. Car, her, bird, fur, for. Schwa. The relaxed, unstressed vowel sound in many syllables of English. The first "a" in "about." The o in lemon. Short vowel. The everyday sound a vowel makes in a closed syllable. The "a" in "cat." The "i" in "sit." Sight word. A word the reader recognizes instantly. Sight words are built through accurate decoding plus repetition, not through visual memorization. Silent-e. The pattern where a single vowel + consonant + final e signals that the first vowel says its long sound. Cake, hope, kite. Syllable. A unit of pronunciation containing one vowel sound. Most multisyllabic words can be split into syllables. Trigraph. Three letters representing one sound. Igh in night. Tch in match. Vowel. Any letter representing a sound made with open air flow. A, E, I, O, U, and sometimes Y. Vowel team. Two or more vowels working together to represent a single sound. Ai, ay, ee, ea, oa, ow, oo, ie, ue, ew.

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 8. Decodable Practice Words by Pattern

Words organized so each set teaches one pattern at a time

Practice each set out loud. Use Map · Blend · Check · Adjust on every word. Move to the next set only when the current set feels easy.

CVC — short a

Closed syllable, short a sound. cat · bat · hat · map · tap · cap · ran · fan · man · pan · bag · tag · wag · jam · ham · can · fat · mat · sad · mad

CVC — short e, i, o, u

Closed syllables, other short vowels. bed · red · leg · get · wet · hit · sit · big · dig · pin · hot · pot · top · rob · mom · bus · cup · mud · run · sun

Consonant blends — initial

Two consonants at the start, each keeping its sound. stop · spin · step · skip · stem · clip · clap · flag · flop · plan · slim · slap · brick · grab · grin · trim · crib · drum · drip · frog

Consonant blends — final

Two consonants at the end, each keeping its sound. bank · tent · band · hand · land · sand · best · fist · list · mist · help · jump · lump · milk · gift · lift · melt · belt · felt · bolt

Consonant digraphs sh, ch, th, wh

Two letters, one sound. ship · shop · shut · fish · wish · dish · chip · chop · chin · such · much · rich · thin · this · that · then · with · whip · when · whiz

Silent-e — long a

VCe pattern, long a sound. cake · bake · take · name · game · same · face · race · gate · late · make · wake · plate · brake · grape · trade · blame · frame · brave · crane

Silent-e — long i, o, u

VCe pattern, other long vowels. bike · like · time · dime · ride · side · note · rope · hole · home · cute · mute · tube · rule · June · stone · slope · spoke · prune · close

Vowel team ee

Two e's, long e sound. see · bee · tree · free · feet · meet · sweet · sleep · sheep · green · screen · queen · cheek · seed · need · feed · speech · keep · peek · steep

Vowel team ea

Most often long e; sometimes short e. eat · sea · tea · read · mean · leaf · seat · beach · clean · dream · head · bread · ahead · spread · steady · ready · heavy · feather · weather · death

Vowel teams ai and ay

Long a. AI in middle, AY at end. rain · pain · main · train · brain · paint · faint · claim · play · day · stay · gray · tray · spray · display · relay · portray · betray · stray · decay

Vowel teams oa and ow

Long o. boat · coat · road · soap · goal · load · toast · coast · snow · low · grow · slow · throw · window · follow · yellow · pillow · tomorrow · shadow · arrow

Diphthongs oi and oy

OI in middle, OY at end. coin · boil · oil · spoil · point · joint · hoist · moist · boy · toy · joy · enjoy · destroy · employ · annoy · decoy · corduroy · convoy · loyal · royal

Diphthongs "ou" and "ow"

OU in middle, OW at end (mostly). out · loud · cloud · found · sound · ground · round · mouth · cow · how · now · down · brown · crown · drown · powder · tower · power · shower · flower

R-controlled ar

Vowel + r dominates. car · far · star · jar · park · dark · mark · hard · yard · arm · farm · barn · start · smart · chart · alarm · harvest · garden · apartment · carnival

R-controlled or

Vowel + r dominates. for · or · corn · born · torn · horn · sport · short · sort · north · order · story · story · forest · forty · report · support · afford · record · force

9. Discussion Guide for Tutors and Teachers

Questions to ask a learner using this book

These are questions for the person guiding a learner — a tutor, a literacy volunteer, a homeschool parent, or a teacher. Use them to assess what the learner has internalized and what to practice next.

Before the learner starts the book

•​ What happens in your body when you meet a word you do not know? •​ Were you ever taught phonics? What do you remember about it? •​ What kind of text most often defeats you? Where do you give up?

After Chapter 1 (What Phonics Actually Is)

•​ In your own words, what is the alphabetic principle? Why does it matter? •​ What is the difference between a letter and a grapheme?

After Chapter 3 (Why Phonics Was Abandoned)

•​ If you were taught with the whole-language method, what about your reading still bears the mark of that instruction? •​ What would you say to a teacher who thinks phonics is "old-fashioned"?

After Chapter 8 (Exceptions That Are Not Really Exceptions)

•​ Which "exception word" surprised you most when you saw it was actually a pattern? •​ Can you name three words that look irregular but follow predictable rules?

After Chapter 12 (Bridge to Decoding)

•​ On a scale of one to ten, how confident are you about decoding a word you have never seen? What would help that number go up? •​ What pattern do you still want more practice with before you move to Volume 3?

Between sessions: where to direct the learner

•​ GENO, the free AI tutor at globalsovereignuniversity.org, is available 24 hours a day in 83 languages. The learner can ask GENO to read passages aloud, define unfamiliar words, or talk through a chapter. •​ The Sovereign Handshake at globalsovereignuniversity.org/sovereign-handshake matches learners with human mentors from the Civilization Builders program. Free. •​ Volume 3, The Reader's Toolkit: Decoding, is the natural continuation when the learner has the basic phonics code working.

The Code That Unlocks Reading—Phonics 10. The Reading Helix

Where this book sits in the series

The Reading Helix is the literacy framework at Global Sovereign University. Eight volumes that progress from the smallest unit of sound to the highest forms of critical reading. No One Reads Alone — the Helix exists so that no reader has to invent the path for themselves.

•​ Volume 1: Sounds Inside the Words. Phonemic awareness — hearing and manipulating the sounds in spoken words. The foundation everything else stands on. •​ Volume 2: The Code That Unlocks Reading. This book. Phonics — learning which letters and letter combinations make which sounds, and how the English code is structured. •​ Volume 3: The Reader's Toolkit — Decoding. Turning phonics knowledge into the skilled act of decoding unfamiliar words in real text. •​ Volume 4: Reading at the Speed of Thought. Fluency — building the speed and phrasing that lets comprehension stay alive while you read •​ Volume 5: Words You Will Need for Life. Vocabulary — building a working store of words across the domains that matter for adult and academic life. •​ Volume 6: Reading for Meaning. Comprehension — the active construction of understanding from text. •​ Volume 7: Reading Like a Citizen. Critical reading — evaluating sources, detecting argument, and reading to decide. •​ Volume 8: Morphones — The Meaning Inside the Word. Morphology at depth — using word parts to unlock words you have never seen. All eight volumes are free at globalsovereignuniversity.org. Request them, read them in whatever order serves you, and ask for help whenever you need it.

11. About Global Sovereign University

Free education, no logins, no ads, no excuses

Global Sovereign University (GSU) is the public name of The Foundation for Global Instruction, a 501(c)(3) nonprofit educational foundation. Everything GSU produces — books, courses, games, podcasts, research articles, the AI tutor, and the human mentor program — is free. There are no logins, no ads, no paywalls. Anyone with internet access has access to all of it. GSU exists to build a bridge to freedom through education, not handouts. The premise is that anyone, at any age, in any country, can lift themselves out of poverty and into capability if they have access to the tools and the dignity to use them.

How to get help with what you read

GENO. GENO is GSU's AI tutor. He is available 24 hours a day in 83 languages. You can talk to him from any page on the GSU website by clicking his widget in the corner. He can help you work through a hard paragraph, define a word, read aloud while you follow along, or simply listen while you practice. A robot you can actually talk to. The Sovereign Handshake. If you would rather talk to a human, the Sovereign Handshake (globalsovereignuniversity.org/sovereign-handshake) matches you with a mentor from the Civilization Builders program — retired teachers, tradespeople, professionals, and citizens who have signed up to help. Free. The Library. Every book GSU publishes is available to read free at globalsovereignuniversity.org/library. You can also request a printed copy of any title and have it mailed to you at no cost.
